Sitting in the Toilet: Leaning on the Left Foot
Ruling with proof on recommended toilet posture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting posture in the toilet and distinguish between sunnah and wajib based on the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Leaning on the left foot in the toilet is sunnah, not wajib, based on the Prophet's teaching.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Taharah chapter regarding the recommended posture when entering the toilet. The Hanafi school classifies this as a sunnah, derived from a hadith reported by Suraqa ibn Ju'sham. Understanding the precise hukm level is essential to avoid mistakenly treating it as an obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sitting in the toilet by leaning on the left foot (raising the right). The ruling is sunnah (recommended).
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Sunan Bayhaqi (vol. 1, p. 156) where the Prophet taught this posture to Suraqa ibn Ju'sham.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain), making it zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'an ya'tamida' (to lean) is interpreted as recommendation (nadb) in Hanafi usul, not obligation, due to absence of qarinah indicating wujub and the principle of ease.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars consider it merely permissible (mubah) rather than sunnah, as they interpret the command as permissibility only.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school holds it as sunnah based on the explicit teaching of the Prophet, and this is the mufta bihi position.
The hadith is authentic enough for recommendation, even if not for obligation.

And he should sit leaning on his left (foot).

Commentary
This ruling recommends sitting in the toilet with the body weight on the left foot and the right foot raised. The author cites a hadith from Suraqa ibn Ju'sham that the Prophet taught this posture. The Hanafi school considers it sunnah because it aids in cleanliness and prevents drops. It is not obligatory; one may sit on the right foot if necessary.

Key Terms

Prohibition of Speaking in the Toilet
Ruling on speaking while using the toilet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on speaking in the toilet, identify the supporting hadith, and apply the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
Speaking in the toilet is disliked; only necessity permits it.

This lesson addresses a common daily scenario: what is the ruling on speaking while using the toilet? The Hanafi school, based on prophetic hadith, considers it makruh tanzihi (disliked) unless there is a genuine need. We will examine the proof and understand the exception for necessity, as well as a special consideration for dhikr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is speaking while in the toilet. The default ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked). The exception is when necessity (darura) is present, which makes it permissible.
Necessity must be genuine, e.g., warning someone of danger.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is a hadith reported by Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: the Prophet ﷺ said that Allah detests two men talking while relieving themselves (Abu Dawood no. 15). Another proof is the Prophet's action of not returning a salam while urinating, indicating speech is not preferred.
Both hadith are ahad (non-mutawatir), so the ruling is not obligatory but disliked.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly mentions 'yatakanu' (talking) and states Allah's detestation (maqt). Detestation implies prohibition, but in Hanafi usul, the level of prohibition depends on the strength of the dalil. Here, detestation leads to makruh tanzihi, not haram.
The word 'yatakanu' is the operative term; it covers all speech except necessity.
4
Apply the exception of necessity
Necessity (darura) can override the disliked ruling. Examples: calling out to warn a blind person of a hazard, or answering a question that must be addressed immediately. The necessity must be genuine and not excessive.
If there is no necessity, speaking remains disliked.
5
Note the nuance about dhikr
Although general speech is makruh tanzihi, mentioning Allah (dhikr) or reciting Quran while in the toilet is considered haram due to disrespect to the divine names in a place of impurity. This is an exception within the exception.
The ruling for dhikr is more severe than for mundane speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتكلم الا لضرورة
And he should not speak except out of necessity.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This ruling prohibits speaking while in the toilet except when absolutely necessary (e.g., to warn someone of danger). The author supports this with a hadith that the Prophet said Allah detests two men talking while relieving themselves, and another hadith that the Prophet did not return a salam while urinating. The Hanafi school considers speaking in the toilet makruh tanzihi (disliked), and haram if it involves mentioning Allah without necessity.

Key Terms
ضرورة (ḍarūra): An unavoidable need that justifies speaking
makruh tanzihi (makrūh tanzīhī): A disliked act that is not sinful but better to avoid; close to permissible but discouraged

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should a person do if someone greets him while he is in the toilet?
A) Respond with salam
B) Respond after exiting and performing wudu
C) Not respond at all
D) Just make a gesture

Key Takeaway
Speaking in the toilet is generally disliked (makruh tanzihi) and only allowed for genuine necessity; reciting Quran or dhikr is haram.

Facing the Qibla in the Toilet: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on facing qibla while relieving oneself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on facing the qibla while relieving oneself and explain the key evidence and the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Facing or turning your back to the qibla in the toilet is prohibitively disliked even inside buildings.

A common question arises: when using a modern toilet inside a building, is it permissible to face the qibla? The Hanafi school holds that it remains prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi), based on general hadith evidence. Imam Shafi'i, however, permits it inside structures, interpreting the hadith as applying only to open spaces. This lesson will walk you through the Hanafi position, its proof, and the practical difference between the two schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that facing (istiqbal) or turning one's back (istidbar) to the qibla while relieving oneself is makruh tahrimi, and that this applies both inside and outside buildings.
The matn says 'wa yukrahu tahriman' – prohibitively disliked.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that inside a building it is permissible due to the presence of a partition that blocks direct facing.
His view is based on analogizing to the concession for toilets with screens or walls.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: the hadith 'لا تستقبلوا القبلة بغائط ولا بول ولا تستدبروها' was understood by some as specific to open spaces, because the need for privacy inside buildings makes the prohibition inoperative.
Some scholars cite the practice of people in Medina who had latrines not facing the qibla, but not as a blanket prohibition inside.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the hadith is general and no exception was made. The Prophet's companions followed this even in their houses, indicating the prohibition includes built structures.
The Hanafi school prioritizes the general wording and does not accept an analogy to break it without specific evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi must avoid facing or turning one's back to the qibla even in a modern indoor toilet, while a Shafi'i may do so.
This affects daily practice for Muslims living in areas where the qibla direction is directly ahead or behind the toilet seat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكره تحريما استقبال القبلة واستدبارها
And it is prohibitively disliked to face the qibla or turn one's back to it.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage states a makruh tahrimi ruling: facing the qibla or turning one's back to it while urinating or defecating is haram. The author explains that the Hanafi school holds this prohibition applies even inside buildings, contrary to Imam Shafi'i who permits it within structures. Evidence includes hadith from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari and Salman al-Farsi which do not mention any exception for buildings. The Hanafi position is based on the general application of the hadith.

Key Terms
استقبال (istiqbāl): Facing the direction of the qibla
استدبار (istidbār): Turning one's back to the qibla
مكروه تحريماً (makrūh taḥrīman): Prohibitively disliked; an act that is close to haram and must be avoided.
تحريماً (taḥrīman): Indicates the level of prohibition (makruh tahrimi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on facing the qibla while defecating inside an indoor toilet?
A) Permissible because the walls serve as a barrier
B) Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked)
C) Sunnah to face the qibla for blessings
D) Mustahab to face east

Key Takeaway
Facing or turning one's back to the qibla while relieving oneself is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) in all circumstances according to the Hanafi school, based on the general evidence of the hadith.
